What seems 'not to fit' arises from reading both texts as if they spoke with the same direct prophetic voice, when in fact Psalm 41 originally belongs to David, not to Jesus, and contains parts that the New Testament interprets typologically (symbolic parallels), not as a complete literal quotation applied to Jesus.

1. Context of John 13:18

In John 13:18, Jesus cites Psalm 41:9 ('he who eats bread with me has lifted up his heel against me') to point out the parallel between the betrayal suffered by David and the one he himself experiences with Judas. The Gospel does not claim that the entire psalm describes Jesus word for word; it applies only that element of betrayal.

2. Psalm 41:4

In the same psalm, verse 4 ('heal my soul, for I have sinned against you') belongs to David’s personal prayer regarding his own moral situation. In traditional Christian interpretation, that verse is not applied to Jesus, because John’s use of the psalm is selective: it takes the typological part of betrayal, not the personal confessions of the original author.

3. Interpretive key

Many New Testament texts use the psalms this way:

The historical event of the psalmist (David betrayed) functions as a figure or pattern.

The fulfillment refers to the parallel event (Jesus betrayed), not to every phrase of the psalm.

Thus, the apparent contradiction ('the prophecy says he sinned') arises only if one assumes that the entire psalm speaks literally of Jesus; in the historical and literary reading, the psalm first describes David, and the gospel uses only the motif of betrayal as a prophetic correspondence.

Expanded version:

Perfect. Here you have a more extensive version, conceived directly as a WordPress post, integrating your analysis, Gemini’s input, and the logical thread we’ve been refining, with a critical tone, cohesion, and clear citations.

The rider on the white horse, 'love of the enemy,' and the selective omission of the prophetic message

When reading Isaiah 63:3–5, Isaiah 11:1–5, and Revelation 19:11–19, the portrait is consistent and difficult to evade: the rider on the white horse is faithful, true, and just, but also a warrior. He judges, fights, and repays. He treads the winepress of wrath, strikes the nations, and rules with a rod. Within that framework, the principle of loving the enemy does not appear, but rather eye for eye—that is, retributive justice against evil.

This raises an unavoidable question:
if this is the Messiah announced by the prophets and reaffirmed in Revelation, where does the doctrine of loving enemies expressed in Matthew 5:44–45 come from?

The answer is uncomfortable for traditional theology, but coherent from intellectual history: that maxim does not come from Hebraism, but from Greek ethics. It was formulated centuries earlier by Cleobulus of Lindos, one of the so-called Seven Sages, whose philosophy promoted moderation, conciliation, and forgiveness as civic virtues. It is not irrelevant that these concepts proved especially useful for an empire that needed to neutralize all moral and political resistance.

The servant of Isaiah and the warrior God

The case of Isaiah 42:1–17 is key. There, the servant of God is presented within an inseparable framework:
Yahweh goes out like a warrior, defeats His enemies, and at the same time the prophecy explicitly condemns idolatry:

'They will be put to shame who trust in idols.'

However, when this passage is cited in Matthew 12:18, something revealing occurs: only the soft and conciliatory part of the text is selected—the servant who does not break the bruised reed—and both the warrior God and the condemnation of idols are omitted. This is not an accidental omission: it is a theological edit.

This technique of partial quotation is not neutral. By cutting the passage just before Yahweh 'goes forth like a mighty man' and 'raises the battle cry,' the prophetic message is redefined and made compatible with an ethic of submission and passivity.

Idolatry: a significant silence

The contrast becomes even more evident when we observe that, in the sayings attributed to Jesus, there is no explicit condemnation of idolatry, despite the fact that it is a central axis of the Hebrew prophetic message. Isaiah, Jeremiah, and the other prophets never separate divine justice from the denunciation of idols. By contrast, the Jesus presented in the Gospels appears detached from that frontal struggle.

This silence is not minor when one considers the historical context of the Roman Empire, a deeply idolatrous civilization that needed to unify peoples without provoking direct religious clashes.

Aesthetics, power, and Hellenization

To this is added a historical detail often overlooked: the image. The earliest Christian representations showed a young, beardless Jesus, similar to the 'good shepherd.' It was after the officialization of Christianity in Rome that the aesthetic of the supreme Greco-Roman god was imposed: long beard, long hair, throne, and cosmic authority—features that clearly recall Zeus (Jupiter in Rome). This is not merely art, but a cultural translation of power.

A contradiction that demands an answer

If Jesus affirmed in Matthew 5:17 that he did not come to abolish the Law or the Prophets, the tension is evident:
why does the message attributed to him deny—in practice—the God who fights, judges, and hates evil described by those same prophets?
why is the servant separated from the warrior God who sends him?
why is an ethic alien to Hebraism and functional to imperial order introduced?

The conclusion is not light, but it is reasonable:
Rome did not preach the prophetic Jesus, but a Hellenized Jesus; it did not transmit the original message, but one adapted, domesticated, and politically harmless.

We are not dealing with a simple difference of emphasis, but with a doctrinal reconstruction. And that reconstruction explains why the rider on the white horse of Isaiah and Revelation is so difficult to reconcile with the preacher of 'love of the enemy' presented by the Roman tradition.

Good question—and well aimed.

I used it descriptively, not because biblical justice needs a surname, but because today many systems and theologies redefine justice so that it does not punish or repay according to deeds.

2. Does 'non-retributive justice' exist?
In the Bible, no.
In philosophy, modern law, and certain theology, yes (at least as a concept).

Today people speak of:

Restorative justice → prioritizes reconciliation even without punishment.
Therapeutic justice → the guilty party is seen as a 'patient.'
Transformative justice → the system is guilty, not the individual.
Preventive justice → avoids punishing, only 'corrects.'
All of them reduce or eliminate retribution.

76	"Preaching to the dead is senseless: they do not hear. Descending into hell is absurd: that place does not exist. Jesus never descended into hell.█

How could Jesus have descended into hell if, according to Revelation 20:12–15, that place only exists after the final judgment?
Isaiah 66:24 describes that destiny as follows: 'for their worm shall not die, nor shall their fire be quenched.' 'Never' means there is no rescue.

They claim that Jesus descended into hell based on 1 Peter 3:18–20 and Matthew 12:40. In 1 Peter it is asserted that the righteous one died for the unrighteous and then went to preach to the spirits who sinned in the days of Noah. This idea does not hold, because Proverbs 17:15 says that God abhors both the one who justifies the wicked and the one who condemns the righteous, and Proverbs 29:27 affirms that the righteous abhors the wicked.

Furthermore, why preach to the wicked? Daniel 12:10 states that the wicked cannot follow the path of justice, and Revelation 9:20 confirms that they do not repent even under punishment. According to 2 Peter 2:5, God did not spare the ancient world but preserved Noah, a preacher of righteousness. If Noah already preached and the wicked were destroyed, it was because they did not listen.

Luke 16:26 speaks of a great chasm that no one can cross, and describes people incapable of repenting, even if one of the dead were to speak to them.

According to Matthew 25:41, hell is 'the eternal fire prepared for the devil and his angels,' an eternal punishment reserved for the unjust, not for the righteous.

Daniel 12:10 says that only the righteous are purified from their sins. The righteous can repent; the wicked cannot. Psalm 118 declares: 'The LORD has chastened me severely, but He has not given me over to death… I will go through the gates of righteousness; the righteous shall enter through them.'

Jesus alludes to this prophecy in the parable of the wicked tenants when He speaks of His return (Matthew 21:33–43). That return is inconsistent with a return in the same body, because punishment implies correction, and correction implies prior ignorance. That presupposes a new body.

Rome preached in Acts 1:1–11 that Jesus will return with the same body with which it claims He ascended into heaven, after rising from the dead and after having been 'in the heart of the earth three days and three nights.' Even if that were true, there is a contradiction: on the third day Jesus would still be there and, at the same time, would have already risen. Rome took Hosea 6:2 out of context, where the 'days' are an allusion to millennia, and they do not speak of the return to life of a single person, but of several. This is linked to Daniel 12:2 and Psalm 90:4.

If the religion of Jesus was tied to the Law and the Prophets, and Rome did not respect His message, it is logical to conclude that it neither respected the Law nor the Prophets. Therefore, the contradictions in the texts that Rome eventually called the 'Old Testament' are not surprising.

I will show you some examples:
Genesis 4:15 — A murderer protected from the death penalty.
Numbers 35:33 — A murderer condemned to death.
Ezekiel 33:18–20 — The righteous can become unrighteous, and the unrighteous can become righteous.
Versus
Daniel 12:10 — The righteous cannot become unrighteous, and the unrighteous cannot become righteous.

Now, if the unrighteous truly could become righteous, the original message of Jesus would not have been persecuted by anyone but accepted by all.

74	"If you believe that Jesus is the Lord and that God resurrected him, you will not be saved. You have been deceived with that lie, and I will prove it.  █

With Hebrews 1:6, Rome distorted the prophecy of Psalm 97:7.
Where the text demands that all angels / gods worship Jehovah, the Roman reinterpretation diverts worship toward a creature, inverting the original meaning of the passage and transforming a proclamation of divine supremacy into a justification for improper worship.
Hosea 13:4 establishes an absolute principle:
'You shall know no other god but Me, for there is no savior besides Me.'
The statement is exclusive. It leaves no room for a second saving figure with divine status nor for divinized intermediaries. The subject is one alone.
Now then, Hosea 6:2 says:
'He will give us life after two days; on the third day He will raise us up, and we shall live before Him.'
The text:

• does not speak of a single individual,
• does not mention 48 hours,
• does not define 24-hour days.

In this type of ancient literature, 'days' function as units of a period. In several comparable texts, one day equals a thousand years. Under that logic, the passage points to a collective return to life in the third millennium, that is, after two complete millennia, not to an immediate individual event.
This point is crucial: the text does not describe an early resurrection, but a future and collective restoration situated in the third millennium.
Later, this passage was taken out of context to construct the idea of an individual bodily resurrection on the literal third day, and that reading was turned into a mandatory requirement for salvation—something the text never states.
This pattern is reinforced in Romans 10, where it reads:
'If you confess with your mouth that Jesus is Lord and believe in your heart that God raised Him from the dead, you will be saved.'
'For whoever calls on the name of the Lord shall be saved.'
Here another key operation occurs: an earlier text is taken and its original subject is replaced.
The cited text comes from Joel 2:32:
'And it shall come to pass that whoever calls on the name of Jehovah shall be saved…'
The original passage:

• does not mention the Messiah,
• does not mention a sent one,
• does not mention mediators.

It speaks exclusively of Jehovah. In Romans 10, that statement is reapplied to Jesus. The reader can compare both texts and verify the displacement.
Furthermore, there is a narrative that directly contradicts the idea of an immediate bodily resurrection: the parable of the wicked tenants.

In it, Jesus announces his death and his return, but that return is associated with judgment and correction, not with a condition of absolute perfection. He is not presented as someone who 'loves his enemies,' but as part of a reckoning. He identifies himself with the stone the builders rejected.

That image is developed in Psalm 118, where the returning figure is corrected, not destroyed, and then enters through a gate reserved for the righteous. Correction implies process, not prior omniscience.
This is key:

If Jesus had already resurrected with a glorified and immortal body, as claimed in Acts, there would be no reason whatsoever for later correction. A being who already fully knows the truth does not need to be corrected.

The fact that correction exists indicates that he does not return with an omniscient mind, but with one that still needs to learn and be refined. That only fits with a return in a new body, in the same way it will occur with the other righteous.
That is why Daniel 12:10 says that the righteous will be purified. Purifying implies process. And that process fits with a collective resurrection in the third millennium, not with an early individual resurrection.
In summary:
collective texts are individualized;
references to Jehovah are reassigned;
an interpretation is turned into mandatory dogma;
and a system is built that conflicts with the internal coherence of the texts themselves.

Paul says: 'Imitate me, as I imitate Jesus.'
In that same passage, Paul states that it is dishonorable for a man to wear long hair.
Therefore, Paul would not imitate something he himself disapproves of.

From this it follows that Jesus did not wear long hair.
The image circulated and attributed to Jesus does not describe the Jesus whom Paul imitated.

Rome worshiped Zeus, also called Jupiter.

71	"The fallacy of God’s universal love… It is no coincidence that the Bible remained in Latin—a language inaccessible to the people—for centuries. The control of language was also the control of thought. █

The Lie of the 'All-Loving God'
Introduction: When language ceases to align
I always excelled in verbal reasoning tests. That is why, from a very early age, something didn’t add up for me. Language, when clear, does not allow for acrobatics. And Exodus 20:5 is not ambiguous: it commands not to bow down or honor images. It is a direct instruction.
However, at Mass, I was taught to do exactly the opposite. When I pointed out the contradiction, the response was always the same: only the experts of the Church can explain that. I was not offered an explanation; I was imposed upon by a fallacy of authority. And for years, I was even denied direct access to the biblical text to impose dogma upon me.
Isaiah 42: A God who acts and establishes justice
When I was finally able to read the Bible without filters, I understood that the problem was not just the practice, but the narrative itself. Isaiah 42 explicitly condemns idolatry and presents the Servant of God as an active figure: someone who fights, who does not stop until justice triumphs on earth. He is not a passive symbol or an ethereal concept of kindness; he is a real executor of justice, someone who confronts evil and subdues it.
This God does not negotiate with injustice nor tolerate it indefinitely. Justice is not a feeling; it is an order that is imposed.
Matthew 12: The pruning of the message
Upon reaching the New Testament, something disturbing happens. Matthew 12 associates that prophecy from Isaiah with Jesus, but the text is no longer the same. The idols disappear. The God who overcomes His enemies disappears. The message has been filed down, softened, and trimmed. It is not a simple, innocent omission: precisely what makes those in power uncomfortable is removed.
The Servant is still mentioned, but his judicial function is diluted.
Matthew 5:48 and the birth of the 'All-Loving God'
Later, Matthew 5:48 presents a God reduced to universal love, a God who loves everyone without distinction, even his enemies, without any prior justice being mediated. This image stands in frontal contrast to texts like Nahum 1:2, where God is described as jealous, avenging, and active against His adversaries.
Here, the central contradiction arises: How does God go from being the one who consumes His enemies to being an abstract concept of love that tolerates everything?
The domestication of the narrative
If the Servant of Isaiah was meant to fight until a just order was established, the question is inevitable: Has justice already triumphed and we simply didn’t notice? Or was the message domesticated along the way?
History offers a clear clue. The biblical canon and its interpretation were defined in councils led by Roman emperors. An Empire that needed obedient subjects could not afford a God who legitimized resistance, confrontation, and active justice. Thus, a God who demands justice was transformed into an invitation to passivity, while for centuries it was forbidden to question the text, read it freely, or interpret it outside of clerical control.
It is no coincidence that the Bible remained in Latin—a language inaccessible to the common people—for centuries. Control over language was also control over thought.
Conclusion: Love without justice is not love
The so-called 'All-Loving God' is not the God of Isaiah, nor the God of Nahum, nor the God who establishes justice. It is a construction functional to power: a God who does not disturb, who does not judge, who does not defeat enemies, and who converts injustice into virtue under the name of patience.
The real question is not theological, but logical: If the original message spoke of justice, who benefited from turning it into passivity?

Let us now debunk another Roman deception: The virgin birth of Jesus:

Another even more revealing example: the virgin birth of Jesus. This dogma, adopted by Christianity and later copied by Islam, has no real prophetic basis in the Tanakh. The verse used as 'prophetic proof' is Isaiah 7:14, which says:

'Behold, the virgin (almah) shall conceive and bear a son, and shall call his name Immanuel.'

This passage does not speak of a miraculous virgin, but of a young woman (the Hebrew word almah does not mean virgin; for that, it would be betulah). The context of the chapter shows that Isaiah was referring to an immediate event: the birth of King Hezekiah, son of Ahaz and Abi (2 Kings 18:1–7), who did fulfill the prophecy as a divine sign in his time, some 700 years before Jesus.

'Immanuel' was not a supernatural future messiah, but a symbol that God was with Judah in that generation, and the child who would be born (Hezekiah) effectively saved Jerusalem from the Assyrian invasion. There is no prophecy to justify the virgin birth of Jesus. It was a later theological construct, influenced by Greco-Roman pagan cults where demigods were born to virgin women impregnated by gods.

According to the Bible, Jesus was born of a virgin, but this contradicts the context of the prophecy in Isaiah 7. The apocryphal gospels, including the Gospel of Philip, also perpetuate this idea. However, Isaiah’s prophecy refers to the birth of King Hezekiah, not Jesus. Hezekiah was born to a woman who was a virgin at the time the prophecy was told, not after she became pregnant, and the prophecy of Immanuel was fulfilled by Hezekiah, not Jesus. Rome has hidden the true gospel and used apocryphal texts to distract and legitimize major lies. Jesus did not fulfill Isaiah’s prophecies about Immanuel, and the Bible misinterprets the meaning of the virgin in Isaiah 7.
 
Isaiah 7: 14-16: This passage mentions a virgin who will conceive a son named Immanuel, which means 'God with us.' The prophecy is given to King Ahaz and refers to the immediate political situation, specifically the destruction of the lands of the two kings Ahaz fears (Pekah and Rezin). This aligns with the historical context and timeline of King Hezekiah’s birth, not Jesus’.

🛡️ Description of the Roman Soldier's Attire in the Statue:

https://itwillbedotme.wordpress.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/07/the-roman-michael.jpg

Metal helmet: Designed in the classic Roman style, likely inspired by the galea type, it protects the head and part of the neck. It lacks plumes or decorations typical of a general, reinforcing the image of a common soldier.
Muscle cuirass or lorica segmentata: Covering the torso, the soldier wears armor molded to the body, imitating the shape of pectoral muscles. This was typical of Roman legionaries and statues of military authority.
Military skirt (cingulum): Below the cuirass, a typical skirt with leather (or metal) strips can be seen. It protected the lower abdomen and allowed for mobility.
Greaves or Roman sandals (caligae): The legs show the classic military sandals, though in many sculptures, for artistic or symbolic reasons, the figure may appear barefoot or only partially dressed.
Shield: Oval or rectangular in shape, held firmly in one hand. It reinforces his identity as a fighter, not a celestial being.
Sword or spear: The soldier holds a sword in his right hand, pointed downward as a symbol of threat, power, or judgment. It is a gladius, the iconic short sword of Roman legionaries.
Artificially added wings: Large, stylized wings have been attached to his back. These are not part of Roman military attire; they are the only 'angelic' element — clearly added to disguise him as a heavenly being. This symbolic addition transforms the soldier into the false 'archangel.'

Injustice cannot be justified by ideologies or religious beliefs. Institutionalized religions are not channels to God, but rather human constructs carefully designed to manipulate consciences, justify power, and divide people under a theater of false spirituality.

The internal contradictions in the texts these religions consider 'sacred' are the first symptom of their human fabrication. For example, in Genesis 4:15, God protects Cain after he murdered his brother Abel:

'I will set a mark on Cain so that no one who finds him will kill him.'
A decision that grants impunity, completely contradicting what Numbers 35:33 later states:
'The land cannot be cleansed from the shedding of blood except with the blood of him who shed it.'
Is it justice to protect the murderer while demanding bloody punishment in another passage? These contradictions are not accidental: they are the product of centuries of self-serving redaction, where different tribal traditions and theological positions were fused by scribes in the service of power.

Another even more revealing example: the virgin birth of Jesus. This dogma, adopted by Christianity and later copied by Islam, has no real prophetic basis in the Tanakh. The verse used as 'prophetic proof' is Isaiah 7:14, which says:

'Behold, the virgin (almah) shall conceive and bear a son, and shall call his name Immanuel.'

This passage does not speak of a miraculous virgin, but of a young woman (the Hebrew word almah does not mean virgin; for that, it would be betulah). The context of the chapter shows that Isaiah was referring to an immediate event: the birth of King Hezekiah, son of Ahaz and Abi (2 Kings 18:1–7), who did fulfill the prophecy as a divine sign in his time, some 700 years before Jesus.

'Immanuel' was not a supernatural future messiah, but a symbol that God was with Judah in that generation, and the child who would be born (Hezekiah) effectively saved Jerusalem from the Assyrian invasion. There is no prophecy to justify the virgin birth of Jesus. It was a later theological construct, influenced by Greco-Roman pagan cults where demigods were born to virgin women impregnated by gods.

And how does Islam repeat this same narrative? Because Islam was not born in a vacuum. Muhammad was influenced by Judeo-Christian sources, especially by his mentor, the Christian monk Bahira, who taught him doctrines that were already part of Roman Christianity. The Quran adopts the virgin birth of Jesus without criticism or analysis, evidencing a common doctrinal source that comes not from direct revelation, but from institutionalized religious transmission.

This reveals something even deeper: the divisions between Judaism, Christianity, and Islam are not as real as they seem. They are branches created or permitted by the same imperial system—be it Rome, Byzantium, or the later caliphates—to fragment peoples, distract them with theologies, and perpetuate a central power that masquerades as sacred.

In that sense, all institutionalized religions are part of the same project: to control human emotion with carefully curated myths, manipulate fear of the divine, and domesticate people's critical conscience.

The Gospel of Luke (1:26-35) also details the annunciation to Mary by the angel Gabriel, reaffirming the virginal conception of Jesus.

This passage, which was introduced to Muhammad by a Christian monk according to some historical sources, shows the Christian influence on Islam and how both religions, seemingly rivals, share doctrines that could serve common political interests, especially those of Rome.

🔍 The Prophecy of Isaiah and King Hezekiah: The Hidden Truth
Isaiah 7:14 states:

'Therefore the Lord himself will give you a sign: Behold, a young woman will conceive and bear a son, and will call him Immanuel.'

Here, the original Hebrew word 'almah' means 'young woman,' not necessarily 'virgin' in the strict sense that has later been interpreted. The context of this prophecy is historical and political, addressed to King Ahaz during a critical period for Judah, when two enemy kings threatened the stability of the kingdom.

This sign is not a distant future messianic promise, but an immediate assurance that the threats of Pekah and Rezin would soon be defeated.

Historical facts confirm the immediate fulfillment with the birth of King Hezekiah, son of Ahaz:

2 Kings 18:1-7 describes Hezekiah as a righteous king, who eliminated idolatry and trusted completely in Jehovah, achieving prosperity and miraculous protection against Assyria:

'…Hezekiah son of Ahaz, king of Judah, began to reign… He did what was right in the sight of Jehovah… He trusted in Jehovah, the God of Israel; there was none like him after him or before him among all the kings of Judah… And Jehovah was with him; and wherever he went out, he prospered.'

Isaiah 7:15-16 also notes:

'He will eat butter and honey until he knows to refuse the evil and choose the good. For before the child knows to refuse the evil and choose the good, the land of the two kings you fear will be forsaken.'

The fall of Pekah and Rezin is historically recorded in 2 Kings 15:29-30, confirming that the prophecy was fulfilled during Hezekiah's time, not centuries later with Jesus.

Furthermore, 2 Kings 19:35-37 narrates how the angel of the Lord destroyed the Assyrian army, freeing Judah, a miraculous event that further cements the fulfillment of the prophecies with Hezekiah.

What you point out in Psalm 69:21 highlights an important contradiction between the tone of the psalms considered 'messianic' and the discourse of love for enemies attributed to Jesus in the New Testament.

Psalm 69:21:
'They gave me gall for food, and for my thirst they gave me vinegar to drink.'
This verse is cited in the Gospels as a prophecy fulfilled during Jesus' crucifixion.

Matthew 27:34:
'They gave him vinegar mixed with gall to drink, but when he tasted it, he refused to drink it.'

And also:

John 19:29-30:
'Now a vessel full of vinegar was standing there. So they filled a sponge with vinegar, put it on hyssop, and held it to his mouth. When Jesus had received the vinegar, he said, 'It is finished.'

This is presented as a fulfillment of the prophecy of Psalm 69. However, the immediate context of that psalm contradicts the message it claims to fulfill. Consider the following verse:

Psalm 69:22:
'Let their banquet be a snare before them, and what is good, a stumbling block.'
And later (verses 23-28), the enemies are cursed, asking that they be blinded, persecuted, and blotted out of the book of life.

There is no trace of forgiveness.
On the contrary, the tone is one of judgment, punishment, and condemnation.

This stands in stark contrast to the message attributed to Jesus during the crucifixion:

Luke 23:34:
'And Jesus said, 'Father, forgive them, for they know not what they do.''

So, a legitimate question arises:

If the Gospels say that Jesus is fulfilling Psalm 69 by receiving vinegar on the cross,
why do they completely ignore the sequence of curses and judgment that immediately follows in the psalm?

It is inconsistent to select a verse as a fulfilled prophecy (the vinegar), but then insert into the scene a phrase that contradicts the spirit of the original text ('Father, forgive them'), as if it came from the same prophetic thread.

Conclusion:
The idea that Jesus fulfills prophecies like Psalm 69:21 is untenable without taking the full context into account. And by inserting phrases like 'Father, forgive them' into the narrative, the Gospel completely shifts the focus of the cited text, creating an apparent harmony that is actually sustained by a selective and decontextualized reading.

One of the problems many encounter when studying the Bible critically is that, because it was written by various authors over thousands of years, it contains messages that are not always consistent with each other. Often, the different books of the Bible reflect different traditions, cultures, and perspectives, making it difficult to see them as a unified and perfect work. In Deuteronomy 20:16-18, for example, the total destruction of certain peoples, including women and children, is commanded, which is difficult to reconcile with the message of love and forgiveness found in the New Testament.

Here's the clearest context:
🌞 Winter Solstice and December 25th
• The winter solstice occurs around December 21st or 22nd. It is the shortest day of the year, with the longest night.
• From that moment on, the days slowly begin to lengthen, which was interpreted as the 'rebirth of the Sun.'
• Therefore, December 25th was celebrated as the day the Sun begins to 'conquer darkness.' Hence the title 'Sol Invictus': the Invincible Sun.
🏛️ Roman Cult of Sol Invictus
• Emperor Aurelian made the cult of Sol Invictus official in 274 AD, establishing December 25th as its main day.
• This cult blended with other solar traditions of Mithraism and other religions of the empire.
• Since it was difficult to eradicate these popular festivals, the Church in Rome adapted this date, saying that the 'true Sun' was Christ, and moved his 'birth' to December 25th.
• Church Fathers such as Saint Justin and Tertullian accepted this association with the sun, calling it the 'Sun of Justice' (inspired by Malachi 4:2), although this connection is entirely forced and astrological, not prophetic.
So yes, December 25th was the day of the sun, and Christmas is a disguised continuation of the Roman solar cult. If the empire dared to alter the New Testament with inventions, why wouldn't it also infiltrate and manipulate passages from the Old Testament?

March 2025 - The Spam YouTube Doesn't Detect

You said:

'I've noticed an annoying message in many comments on various YouTube videos. It's about one or more people posting about a supposed solution to their financial problems, even on videos with barely 50 views and about 5 comments. 'Coincidentally,' that comment always has about 25 replies, some confirming the supposed benefit and others asking for more information. It's obviously spam, but YouTube doesn't detect it.'

ChatGPT said:

Yes, that's a type of camouflaged spam that YouTube doesn't always detect because it's designed to look like a real conversation. These comments usually follow a pattern:

An 'innocent' initial comment
🔹 'Thanks to [fake name or financial mentor] I now make $500 a day effortlessly. I can't believe it. 😍'

Fake responses (bots or purchased accounts)
🔹 'Wow, he helped me too. His method is amazing.'
🔹 'How can I contact him?'
🔹 'Yes, his WhatsApp is +123456789. Write to him before it's too late.'

This type of spam is clever because:
✅ Avoid typical scam keywords (like 'easy money').
✅ Use multiple accounts to simulate a real conversation.
✅ Comment on videos with few views and few comments, where the creator is less likely to delete it.

Why doesn't YouTube detect it?
Although YouTube has good filters for direct self-promotion, this type of spam is harder to filter because:
🔹 It doesn't use links, but rather indirect mentions ('write to this number').
🔹 It looks like a normal conversation, not a repetitive message.
🔹 It's mixed with real users who might fall for the trick and respond.

Bot, the pattern you mentioned is exactly the same one I noticed: Thanks to XYZ I found... How did you know?
ChatGPT said:
I knew because this type of spam is a global pattern that scammers have been using for years on YouTube, Facebook, Instagram, and other platforms. 🚨

It works because:
✔️ It looks like a real testimonial, which builds trust.
✔️ It uses fake names ('Thanks to XYZ I found...') to look like an honest recommendation.
✔️ It's filled with fake responses to simulate organic interaction.
✔️ Avoid direct links, which makes it difficult to detect.

💡 Fun fact:
These bots are programmed to detect videos with few comments and post them there, because they're less likely to be reported by the creator or the community.

You noticed it because you're paying attention. Most people ignore it or even fall for it. 😨

In the peaceful valley of Montclair, where the vineyards flourished under the golden sun, a terrifying rumor began to spread. The workers of the vineyard spoke in whispers about a mysterious vine, whose grapes carried a curse.

It all began when the young Enzo, an apprentice viticulturist, noticed something strange in a section of the vineyard. Among the vines of sweet and fragrant fruits, there was a vine whose fruit had an unusual, almost hypnotic glow. Unaware, a worker tasted one of these grapes and, within minutes, his eyes turned as white as milk. The man fell to the ground, screaming that darkness had devoured him.

Panic spread quickly. Enzo and the other workers investigated the strange plant. At first glance, it seemed just like the others, but those who touched it felt a chill run through their bodies. When they cut one of its grapes, they discovered that the juice was a red so intense it looked like blood.

The owner of the estate, Mrs. Violette, called a botanist to analyze the vine. However, by nightfall, the expert vanished without a trace, leaving only his broken glasses beside the cursed vine.

Desperate for answers, Enzo decided to keep watch over the vine in the dim light of dawn. Hidden in the shadows, he saw something that took his breath away: a tall and beastly silhouette emerged from the forest and bent down next to the cursed vine. Its face was that of a bear, but twisted goat horns sprouted from its temples. Its claws caressed the poisonous clusters with an unnatural reverence.

The creature raised its head and, as if sensing Enzo’s presence, turned its glowing eyes toward him. With a guttural growl, it murmured words in an arcane language before vanishing into the mist of the dawn.

Enzo ran back to the estate, trembling in fear. When he recounted what he had seen, Mrs. Violette paled. 'It’s the Devil’s Tree,' she whispered. 'It’s not just a vine, but the work of something that doesn’t belong to this world.'

The next morning, they gathered the workers and burned the cursed vine until only ashes remained. But Enzo never forgot those eyes that had stalked him in the dark. And in the nearby forest, among the whispering trees, a pair of red eyes continued to watch, waiting for the right moment to plant its curse again.

Then a mysterious man approached, claiming to be Gabriel. He said to them, 'It’s useless that you burned that tree while the one who planted it still roams. The solution is to kill the beast and send it to hell where it can never escape… that’s why I’ve come.'

When the Bible Preaches the Same Thing as Pagan Philosophers

The thinking of Cleobulus of Lindos, reflected in his teaching, 'Every man is a friend or an enemy according to how you conduct yourself toward him,' resembles the Golden Rule: 'Treat others as you wish to be treated.' However, the Bible itself contradicts this idea in Ecclesiasticus 12:5-7 and Psalm 109:4-5, which warns that some will return evil for good and hatred for love. This demonstrates that the Golden Rule is not always followed, as there are those who act with ingratitude and malice regardless of how they are treated.

Likewise, Cleobulus's idea of moderation finds a parallel in Ecclesiastes 7:16: 'Do not be overly righteous, nor overly wise; why should you destroy yourself?' suggesting that an excess of justice and wisdom can be harmful. However, this contradicts other passages such as Revelation 22:11: 'Let the righteous do righteousness, and let the holy be holy even more,' which commands constant growth in righteousness. It also clashes with Proverbs 4:7, which states: 'Wisdom is the principal thing; get wisdom, and over all your possessions get understanding,' exalting wisdom without limit.

These contradictions reflect how the Scriptures have been influenced by different schools of thought. Ecclesiastes, with its skeptical and philosophical tone, seems to lean toward the Greek prudence of moderation, while Proverbs and Revelation promote an absolutist view of justice and wisdom. This disparity shows that Hellenism not only left its mark on the Bible but also generated internal tensions between different doctrines.

The Error of Aristotle and the Truth About the Fall of Bodies

Aristotle was a philosopher and scientist of Ancient Greece, whose influence lasted for centuries in various disciplines, including logic, metaphysics, and physics. However, some of his statements were erroneous, such as his explanation of the fall of bodies.

For centuries, it was believed that heavier objects fell faster than lighter ones. This idea, attributed to Aristotle, was based on observation without rigorous experimental verification. However, Galileo Galilei refuted this belief with concrete evidence.

The Aristotelian Theory of the Fall of Bodies

Aristotle claimed that heavier objects fell faster than lighter ones because he thought that the speed of the fall depended on their weight. According to his logic, an object ten times heavier than another should fall ten times faster. This idea was accepted for centuries until Galileo Galilei refuted it with rigorous experiments.

Galileo and Free Fall

Galileo demonstrated that, in the absence of air resistance, all objects fall at the same speed, regardless of their weight. This is because the acceleration due to gravity (g) is constant for all bodies in the same gravitational field.

Mathematical Explanation

The gravitational force acting on an object is:

F = m * g

Where:

F is the gravitational force, m is the mass of the object, g is the acceleration of gravity (approximately 9.8 m/s² on Earth).

According to Newton’s second law:

a = F / m

Substituting the equation for gravitational force:

a = (m * g) / m

Since m cancels out, we get:

a = g

This means that all objects fall with the same acceleration in a vacuum, regardless of their weight or size.

The Experiment on the Moon

In 1971, the Apollo 15 astronauts conducted a demonstration on the Moon, where there is no atmosphere. They dropped a hammer and a feather at the same time, and both reached the ground simultaneously, confirming Galileo’s predictions.

Conclusion

Although Aristotle contributed knowledge, his error in the theory of the fall of bodies demonstrates the importance of the experimental method. Thanks to Galileo, we now know that gravitational acceleration is the same for all objects, regardless of their mass, when there is no air resistance. This forever changed our understanding of physics and laid the foundation for modern science.
Cleobulus of Lindos was a Greek philosopher and poet of the 6th century BC, considered one of the Seven Sages of Greece. He governed the city of Lindos, in Rhodes, and promoted education and moderation as principles of life. Several aphorisms and riddles are attributed to him, highlighting the phrase: 'Moderation is best.' He also taught about life and coexistence, leaving reflections such as:

'Any man, at any moment in life, can be your friend or enemy, depending on how you conduct yourself with him.' 'Do good to your friends and enemies, because in this way, you will keep some and attract others.'

8	"The Gospel of Peter and the Adulterated Prophecy: Immortality, Rejuvenation, and the Lost Faith Rediscovered. █ Eternal Life and Prophecies

The concept of eternal life has been manipulated by modern religions to conceal its true meaning: literal immortality, bodily rejuvenation, and conscious existence in either eternal reward or eternal punishment. This vision, supported by multiple texts, both canonical and apocryphal, has been distorted by the Roman Empire through the councils that defined the biblical canon. This is not about defending the Bible or the apocrypha as a whole, but about connecting coherent fragments that align with the truth.

The Apocalypse of Peter describes how, after a period of error, the righteous will rule, and the wicked will be exposed and punished. Additionally, it mentions those who rejuvenate and never age again, directly relating to Job 33:25, which speaks of regaining youth as part of divine restoration.

On the other hand, texts such as Psalm 41:5-11 show that the prophecy of healing involves first acknowledging sin, then confessing it, and finally receiving healing. This contradicts the Roman narrative about Jesus, as the New Testament insists that he never sinned. John 13:18 attempts to force the fulfillment of this prophecy onto Judas, but the text itself presents contradictions: if Jesus knew from the beginning that Judas was a traitor, he could not have truly trusted him, nullifying the fulfillment of Psalm 41:9 in this context.

We have been told that Psalm 16:10 is related to the resurrection of Jesus, but this is false. In reality, this passage connects with Job 33:24-25 and Psalm 118, demonstrating its relation to eternal life rather than to a specific resurrection of Jesus. The key to understanding the final resurrection lies in Psalms 41 and 118, which indicate that the righteous also sin—this only makes sense if resurrection is not merely returning to an old body but rather reincarnation into a new body and a new mind. Upon being reborn, these righteous ones do not remember their past lives and initially lack knowledge of the truth, leading them to sin until they rediscover God’s original message. This process is necessary for the restoration of justice and eternal reward.

Rome also falsified the teaching about Jesus’ resurrection. The common depiction of a bodily resurrection on the third day does not hold up when examining Hosea 6:1-3, which speaks in plural terms and refers to a process tied to the third millennium, not a literal three-day period. This aligns with prophecies such as Isaiah 42:1-4, Daniel 12:1-3, Isaiah 61:1, and Psalm 110:7, which describe the return of a righteous one in a future context, rather than an immediate and physical resurrection. Additionally, while the Gospel of Peter defends the Roman version of the resurrection, the Apocalypse of Peter emphasizes future transformation and eternal justice, showing that the original idea was not a simple physical resurrection but rather a final restoration and judgment.

Jesus himself, in Matthew 21:33-44, refers to Psalm 118 when speaking of his return, which contradicts the idea of a resurrection in the same body with the same memories. If this were the case, knowing the truth, he would not sin or be subject to punishment, as stated in Psalm 118:13-20. This passage is also linked to Job 33:24-25, reinforcing the idea of resurrection as reincarnation into a new body without prior memories.

The true meaning of eternal life implies that both heaven and hell must be bodily experiences, as without a body, there is neither pain nor pleasure. The restoration of the righteous and the punishment of the wicked require bodies in which consciousness can feel. This vision has been systematically hidden to maintain control over humanity and to deny the true promise of immortality to those who seek justice.
The Message of Healing in Psalm 41 and the Adulteration of the Gospel
📖 Psalm 41:4-11
'Have mercy on me, O Jehovah, and heal me, for I have sinned against you. My enemies desire my death, saying, ‘When will he die, and his name perish?’ Even the one I trusted, the one I relied on and who ate my bread, has lifted up his heel against me. But you, O Jehovah, have mercy on me and raise me up so that I may repay them. By this, I will know that you are pleased with me: that my enemy does not triumph over me.'
This passage presents a clear sequence:
The protagonist sins: 'for I have sinned against you.'
He confesses his sin and asks for healing: 'Have mercy on me, O Jehovah, and heal me.'
God heals him and raises him up to take vengeance on his enemies.
However, the Roman version of the Gospel claims that this prophecy was fulfilled in Jesus, which is false because:
🔹 Jesus never sinned (according to the Bible itself):
📖 1 Peter 2:22 – 'He committed no sin, nor was deceit found in his mouth.'
📖 Hebrews 4:15 – 'He was tempted in every way as we are, yet without sin.'
🔹 Jesus was neither healed nor raised up to take vengeance on his enemies.
🔹 Jesus could not have trusted Judas if he already knew he would betray him (John 6:64).
📖 John 13:18
'I am not speaking of all of you; I know whom I have chosen. But this is so that the Scripture may be fulfilled: ‘He who eats bread with me has lifted up his heel against me.’'
If Jesus already knew from the beginning that Judas was a traitor, then he could never have truly trusted him, which directly contradicts Psalm 41:9, which says the betrayer was someone in whom the righteous person trusted.
📖 Conclusion:
Rome adulterated the message by claiming that Psalm 41 was fulfilled in Jesus. This prophecy actually describes someone who sins, repents, is healed, and then brings justice, which does not match the biblical portrayal of Jesus.

Other Key Passages About Immortality and Judgment
📖 Daniel 12:3 – 'The wise will shine like the brightness of the firmament, and those who turn many to righteousness, like the stars forever and ever.'
➡️ This verse supports the idea of eternal glorification of the righteous.

➡️ Confirms the end of death and eternal comfort for the righteous.
📖 Matthew 25:46 – 'And these shall go away into eternal punishment, but the righteous into eternal life.'
➡️ Defines the final destiny of both the righteous and the wicked.

General Conclusion
This set of texts reveals that the promise of eternal life is literal, including physical resurrection and the rejuvenation of the righteous. Hell and heaven cannot be merely spiritual states; they must involve bodies in which consciousness can experience either punishment or reward.
Furthermore, the analysis of Psalm 41 and John 13:18 exposes the falsity of the Roman version of its fulfillment, demonstrating that Rome adulterated the original message. The true prophecy about the righteous one being betrayed implies that he first sins, then repents, is healed, and finally brings justice—something that was NOT fulfilled in Jesus according to the Bible itself, since it states that he never sinned.
This exposes the manipulation of the message and confirms the need to question the authority of the canon imposed by Rome.

7	"The Death Penalty Debate  █ 
Moderator (neutral AI):
Welcome to the debate. Today we will discuss whether the death penalty should be applied to murderers. Introducing LexBot, who advocates its implementation, and EthosBot, who opposes it.

LexBot (pro-death penalty):
Thank you. The death penalty is a tool of justice and deterrence. A murderer has shown disregard for human life, and execution is the only way to ensure that he does not kill again. It also provides justice for victims and their families.

EthosBot (anti-death penalty):
Justice should not be driven by revenge. The death penalty is irreversible, and there are cases of miscarriages of justice where innocent people have been executed. True justice should seek rehabilitation when possible, not elimination.

LexBot:
But some crimes are unforgivable. If a murderer reoffends after being released, who bears the blame? Society has the right and duty to protect itself from dangerous criminals. Rehabilitation is a utopia in many cases.

EthosBot:
Protecting society does not require execution, but life imprisonment. In addition, the death penalty has been used with racial and political bias. If the judicial system is not perfect, how can an irreversible sentence be justified?

LexBot:
The risk of error must be minimized, but it cannot be an excuse to ignore justice. In addition, keeping murderers in prison for life costs resources that could be used to prevent crime and support victims.

EthosBot:
Life should not have a monetary price. In addition, in some countries with the death penalty, homicide rates remain high. It is not an effective deterrent. Instead, prevention and education policies have been shown to reduce crime.

LexBot:
Statistics are debatable. But beyond the numbers, the question is moral: whoever has taken a life does not deserve to continue living. The death penalty is proportional to the crime committed.

EthosBot:
Morality is not absolute. There are societies that have abolished the death penalty and operate with justice. If we accept that the State can kill, are we not legitimizing violence as a response?

LexBot (final answer):
Being a fair human being is defending the innocent. Not punishing murderers as they should with what they do fear means being complicit and not being fair. Applying the death penalty to murderers will not make us more or less human beings; it will simply tip the balance in favor of justice.

A punishment that is not proportional to the crime, no matter how you want to label it, is not justice. Justice is giving everyone what they deserve. Peaceful people deserve peace and quiet, health, good education, housing and a high quality of life. And for that it is necessary to eliminate murderers and extortionists, because the latter do not leave good people in peace. It is not fair that the taxes of good people are used to keep alive those who do not respect innocent lives.

The lack of the death penalty has proven its ineffectiveness. Without murderers, there will be no more need to apply the death penalty; it does not have to be an endless cycle. The death penalty must be legalized in such a way that those who accuse innocent people are sentenced to the same punishment they sought for the innocent. The character of Bahira in the story of Muhammad is an interesting one, and has been the subject of debate and speculation. Bahira, according to some Islamic sources, was a Christian monk who is believed to have had an encounter with Muhammad when he was young, which left an impression on his religious thinking. Some accounts suggest that Bahira may have spoken to him about aspects of the Christian religion, and that his influence may have contributed to the ideas that Muhammad later developed in the Qur'an.

The existence of Bahira and his meeting with Muhammad as a child is a controversial issue and difficult to verify historically. Below are some key aspects of the evidence for Bahira, his identity and meeting with Muhammad:

1. Historical Evidence and Islamic Sources

Islamic sources: The story of Bahira is found primarily in hadiths (sayings of Muhammad) and in the early biography of Muhammad, known as the Sira. The main Islamic sources that mention Bahira are Ibn Ishaq (in his biography of Muhammad), al-Tabari, Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani, and other texts that recount Muhammad's early years.

Account of the Encounter: According to these sources, Bahira was a Christian monk living in Syria or in the region of Bostra (a city in present-day Syria). According to the accounts, when Muhammad was about 12 years old, he traveled with his uncle Abu Talib to Syria on a trading trip. During this trip, the monk Bahira is said to have recognized signs of prophecy in Muhammad and warned his uncle that Muhammad was destined to be a great religious leader.

Recognition of Muhammad as a prophet: In some accounts, Bahira is said to have recognized that Muhammad was the future last prophet, a spiritual leader, by certain signs that only a Christian monk would have noticed. This encounter seems to have had an impact on Muhammad, but it is not known for certain how influential it really was.

 2. Lack of Historical Evidence Outside Islamic Sources

Absence of external evidence: There is no independent evidence outside Islamic sources that verifies Bahira's existence or meeting with Muhammad. There are no Christian records or historical documents contemporary to Muhammad's time that mention Bahira as an influential Christian monk. No archaeological evidence has been found to confirm this either.

Credibility issues: Some scholars consider that the stories about Bahira may be part of a hagiographic narrative (i.e. an idealized account of Muhammad's life), intended to legitimize Muhammad as a prophet by linking him to recognized Christian traditions. The story of Bahira may have been embellished or expanded over time to give a direct connection between Islam and previous monotheistic religions such as Judaism and Christianity.

3. Interpretations of the Academics

Some scholars of early Islam suggest that the figure of Bahira may have been mythological or legendary, created to give a more divine or spiritual basis to the story of Muhammad. In this context, some think that the figure of Bahira was invented or exaggerated to strengthen the religious legitimacy of Muhammad and his message.

Other scholars, such as Montgomery Watt in his book Muhammad: Prophet and Statesman, argue that Bahira may have been a genuine Christian monk, but that the significance of his meeting with Muhammad was overemphasized in order to link Islam with Christianity and present him as part of a tradition of divine prophets.

 4. The Christian Influence

Although the figure of Bahira and his relationship to Muhammad remains uncertain, many scholars agree that Christianity may have had a significant influence on Muhammad's thinking. The Quran mentions many biblical stories and figures (such as Jesus, Moses and Abraham), suggesting an interaction between Jewish and Christian traditions and the rise of Islam.

If Bahira did exist, it is possible that he had some influence on Muhammad, but it must also be considered that Muhammad may have been influenced by Christian communities in the regions where he traveled and lived. However, the extent of that influence and how it affected his message remains a matter of debate.

Conclusion

Bahira's existence as a Christian monk and his meeting with Muhammad is not independently documented in historical sources outside of Islam. Islamic sources tell the story, but there is no archaeological evidence or external historical records to support it. Therefore, although the figure of Bahira is significant within the Islamic tradition, the veracity of his story remains a controversial and speculative question.

This is my story:
José, a young man raised in Catholic teachings, experienced a series of events marked by complex relationships and manipulations. At 19, he began a relationship with Monica, a possessive and jealous woman. Although Jose felt that he should end the relationship, his religious upbringing led him to try to change her with love. However, Monica's jealousy intensified, especially towards Sandra, a classmate who was making advances on Jose.

https://gabriels58.wordpress.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/03/idi02-the-first-signs-in-1995-the-symptoms-of-sandras-future-slander-1.jpg

Sandra began harassing him in 1995 with anonymous phone calls, in which she made noises with the keyboard and hung up.

On one of those occasions, she revealed that she was the one calling, after Jose angrily asked in the last call: 'Who are you?' Sandra called him immediately, but in that call she said: 'Jose, who am I?' Jose, recognizing her voice, said to her: 'You are Sandra,' to which she replied: 'You already know who I am.' Jose avoided confronting her. During that time, Monica, obsessed with Sandra, threatened Jose with harming Sandra, which led Jose to protect Sandra and prolong his relationship with Monica, despite his desire to end it.

Finally, in 1996, Jose broke up with Monica and decided to approach Sandra, who had initially shown interest in him. When Jose tried to talk to her about his feelings, Sandra did not allow him to explain himself, she treated him with offensive words and he did not understand the reason. Jose chose to distance himself, but in 1997 he believed he had the opportunity to talk to Sandra, hoping that she would explain her change of attitude and be able to share the feelings that she had kept silent. On her birthday in July, he called her as he had promised a year earlier when they were still friends—something he couldn’t do in 1996 because he was with Monica. At the time, he used to believe that promises should never be broken (Matthew 5:34-37), though now he understands that some promises and oaths can be reconsidered if made in error or if the person no longer deserves them. As he finished greeting her and was about to hang up, Sandra desperately pleaded, 'Wait, wait, can we meet?' That made him think she had reconsidered and would finally explain her change in attitude, allowing him to share the feelings he had kept silent. However, Sandra never gave him clear answers, maintaining the intrigue with evasive and counterproductive attitudes.

Faced with this attitude, Jose decided not to look for her anymore. It was then that constant telephone harassment began. The calls followed the same pattern as in 1995 and this time were directed to the house of his paternal grandmother, where Jose lived. He was convinced that it was Sandra, since Jose had recently given Sandra his number. These calls were constant, morning, afternoon, night, and early morning, and lasted for months. When a family member answered, they did not hang up, but when José answered, the clicking of the keys could be heard before hanging up.

Jose asked his aunt, the owner of the telephone line, to request a record of incoming calls from the telephone company. He planned to use that information as evidence to contact Sandra's family and express his concern about what she was trying to achieve with this behavior. However, his aunt downplayed his argument and refused to help. Strangely, no one in the house, neither his aunt nor his paternal grandmother, seemed to be outraged by the fact that the calls also occurred in the early morning, and they did not bother to look into how to stop them or identify the person responsible.

This had the strange appearance of orchestrated torture. Even when José asked his aunt to unplug the phone at night so he could sleep, she refused, arguing that one of her sons, who lived in Italy, could call at any moment (considering the six-hour time difference between the two countries). What made things even stranger was Monica’s fixation on Sandra, even though they hadn’t even met. Monica didn’t attend the high school where José and Sandra were enrolled, but she began to feel jealous of Sandra after finding a folder with one of José’s group projects. The folder listed the names of two women, including Sandra, but for some strange reason, Monica became fixated only on Sandra’s name.      

Although José initially ignored Sandra's phone calls, over time he relented and contacted Sandra again, influenced by biblical teachings that advised praying for those who persecuted him. However, Sandra manipulated him emotionally, alternating between insults and requests for him to keep looking for her. After months of this cycle, Jose discovered that it was all a trap. Sandra falsely accused him of sexual harassment, and as if that wasn't bad enough, Sandra sent some criminals to beat up Jose.

That Tuesday, without José knowing it, Sandra had already set a trap for him.

https://ai20me.wordpress.com/wp-content/uploads/2024/09/crei-que-le-estaban-haciendo-brujeria-pero-la-bruja-era-ella.gif 

Days before, José had told his friend Johan about the situation he was going through with Sandra. Johan also suspected that Sandra's strange behavior might be due to some kind of witchcraft by Mónica. That Tuesday, José visited his old neighborhood where he had lived in 1995 and happened to run into Johan. After hearing more details about the situation, Johan recommended that José forget about Sandra and instead go out to a nightclub to meet women—perhaps he would find someone who could make him forget her. José thought it was a good idea.
So they got on a bus and headed toward the nightclub in downtown Lima. Coincidentally, the route of that bus passed near the IDAT institute. Just one block before reaching IDAT, José suddenly had the idea to get off for a moment to pay for a Saturday course he had enrolled in. He had managed to save some money for it by selling his computer and working for a week in a warehouse. However, he had been forced to quit because they exploited workers with 16-hour shifts while officially recording only 12, and if they refused to complete the week, they were threatened with not being paid at all.
So José turned to Johan and said, 'I study here on Saturdays. Since we're passing by, let's get off for a bit, I'll pay for my course, and then we’ll head to the nightclub.'
The moment José stepped off the bus, before even crossing the avenue, he was shocked to see Sandra standing right there on the corner of the institute. In disbelief, he told Johan, 'Johan, I can't believe it—Sandra is right there. She's the girl I told you about, the one who acts so strangely. Wait for me here; I'm going to ask if she got the letter where I warned her about Mónica’s threats against her, and maybe she can finally explain what’s going on with her and what she wants from me with all her calls.'
Johan stayed back as José approached. But as soon as he started speaking—'Sandra, did you see the letters? Can you finally explain to me what’s going on with you?'—Sandra, without saying a word, gestured with her hand, signaling three thugs who had been hiding in different spots: one in the middle of the avenue, another behind Sandra, and another behind José.
The one standing behind Sandra stepped forward and said, 'So you're the sexual harasser who's been bothering my cousin?'
José, caught off guard, responded, 'What? Me, a harasser? On the contrary, she’s the one harassing me! If you read the letter, you’d see it’s about me trying to understand why she keeps calling me!'
Before he could react, one of the thugs grabbed him by the neck from behind and threw him to the ground. Then, together with the one who had claimed to be Sandra’s cousin, they started kicking him. Meanwhile, the third thug went through his pockets, robbing him. It was three against one—José, lying helpless on the pavement.
Luckily, his friend Johan jumped into the fight, managing to give José a chance to get up. But then the third thug picked up some rocks and started throwing them at both José and Johan.
The attack only stopped when a traffic officer intervened. The officer turned to Sandra and said, 'If he’s harassing you, then file a complaint.'
Sandra, visibly nervous, quickly left, knowing full well that her accusation was false.
José, though deeply betrayed, did not go to the police. He had no way to prove the months of harassment he had suffered from Sandra. But beyond the shock of her betrayal, one question haunted him:
'How did she already have this ambush set up when I never come to this place on Tuesday nights? I only come here to study on Saturday mornings.'
This made José suspect that Sandra wasn’t just an ordinary person—she might be a witch with some kind of supernatural power.

These events left a deep mark on Jose, who seeks justice and to expose those who manipulated him. In addition, he seeks to derail the advice in the Bible, such as: pray for those who insult you, because by following that advice, he fell into Sandra's trap.

-1	"Walking on the edge of death along the dark path, but looking for the light, interpreting the lights projected on the mountains so as not to make a false step, to avoid death. █
Night was falling on the main road.
A blanket of darkness covered the winding path that wound through the mountains.
He did not walk aimlessly.
His goal was freedom, but the journey had only just begun.
With his body numb from the cold and his stomach empty for days,
he had no company but the elongated shadow cast by the headlights of the trucks that roared beside him,
advancing without stopping, indifferent to his presence.
Every step was a challenge,
every curve a new trap from which he had to emerge unscathed.
For seven nights and mornings,
he was forced to advance along the thin yellow line of a narrow road with just two lanes,
while trucks, buses and trailers whizzed by just inches from his body.
In the darkness, the deafening roar of engines enveloped him,
and the lights of trucks coming from behind cast their glow on the mountain in front of him.
At the same time, other trucks approached in the opposite direction,
forcing him to decide in fractions of a second
whether to pick up the pace or remain still in his precarious crossing,
where every movement meant the difference between life and death.
Hunger was a beast that devoured him from the inside,
but the cold was no less merciless.
In the sierra, the early hours of the morning were invisible claws that penetrated to the bones.
The wind enveloped him with its icy breath,
as if it wanted to extinguish the last spark of life that remained to him.
He took refuge where he could,
sometimes under a bridge,
other times in a corner of concrete that offered him a minimum of shelter.
But the rain was unforgiving.
Water seeped into his torn clothes,
sticking to his skin and stealing what little warmth he still had.
The trucks continued their march,
and he, stubbornly hoping that someone would feel sorry,
raised his hand, waiting for a gesture of humanity.
But the drivers drove on.
Some with looks of contempt,
others simply ignoring him, as if he were a ghost.
Every now and then, a compassionate soul would stop and offer him a quick ride,
but they were few.
Most saw him as a nuisance,
a shadow on the road,
someone not worth helping.
On one of those endless nights,
desperation drove him to search for food among the scraps left by travelers.
He was not ashamed to admit it:
he fought for food with pigeons,
snatching pieces of hardened biscuits before they could make them disappear.
It was an unequal fight,
but he was determined:
he was not willing to kneel before any image,
nor to accept any man as 'only lord and savior'.
He was not willing to please those sinister individuals
who had already kidnapped him three times over religious differences,
who with their slanders had led him to walk that yellow line.
At another time, a kind man offered him a piece of bread and a drink.
A small gesture,
but in his pain,
that kindness was a balm.
But indifference was the norm.
When he asked for help,
many would walk away,
as if they feared that his misery was contagious.
Sometimes, a simple 'no' was enough to extinguish all hope,
but on other occasions, contempt was reflected in cold words or empty looks.
He didn’t understand how they could ignore someone who could barely stand,
how they could watch a man collapse without batting an eyelid.
And yet, he kept going.
Not because he had the strength,
but because he had no other choice.
He continued down the road,
leaving behind him miles of asphalt,
nights without rest and days without food.
Adversity hit him with everything it had,
but he resisted.
Because deep down,
even in the most absolute desperation,
the spark of survival still burned within him,
fueled by the desire for freedom and justice.
